https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/27-deobfuscating-300x41.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/27-deobfuscating-768x104.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/27-deobfuscating-180x24.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/27-deobfuscating-605x82.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/27-deobfuscating-297x40.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 27</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Deobfuscating the Malware</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>We will unwrap these steps in reverse order below.</p>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Unpacking</h4>



<p>The packer used on this executable employs a simple technique of base64 encoding the PE file and breaking it up into numerous lines which are then embedded into the resource section of the .Net packer stub file. At runtime, the packer reverses this process, then invokes the resulting .Net assembly from memory.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="527" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAABAAAAAIPAQMAAADXVT4vAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAFhJREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14MH2AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAMBbCZ4AAY6s8bYAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="Figure 21: Base64 strings found in the resource section of the packed executable." class="wp-image-27809 lazyload" title="Figure 28"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-1024x527.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-1024x527.png 1024w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-300x154.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-768x395.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-180x93.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-605x311.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-297x153.png 297w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware.png 1182w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="1024" data-eio-rheight="527" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="527" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-1024x527.png" alt="Figure 21: Base64 strings found in the resource section of the packed executable." class="wp-image-27809" title="Figure 28" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-1024x527.png 1024w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-300x154.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-768x395.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-180x93.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-605x311.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware-297x153.png 297w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/28-unpacking-malware.png 1182w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 28</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Base64 strings found in the resource section of the packed executable.</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Extracting this packed code reveals a .Net assembly which is yet another layer of code protection applied using a ‘crypter’. This binary has the following hashes:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>MD5</th><th>SHA256</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>a4f1f4921bb11ff9d22fad89b19b155d</td><td>d81ec563387e2ea47bc8ed50fd36e1de955cb2331d6eaae9f966b5d7ab094806</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 4</strong></figcaption></figure>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Decrypting</h4>



<p>This executable is stub code which performs in-memory AES decryption of a base64 encoded string variable. This string variable holds an encrypted copy of the NanoCore RAT binary.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="649" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAABAAAAAKJAQMAAAAfvMnqAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAGhJREFUeNrtwTEBAAAAwqD1T20MH6AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAADOBkcYAAGegDqzAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="Figure 22: the AES decryption routines" class="wp-image-27812 lazyload" title="Figure 29"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-1024x649.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-1024x649.png 1024w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-300x190.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-768x487.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-180x114.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-537x340.png 537w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-297x188.png 297w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption.png 1446w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="1024" data-eio-rheight="649" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="649" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-1024x649.png" alt="Figure 22: the AES decryption routines" class="wp-image-27812" title="Figure 29" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-1024x649.png 1024w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-300x190.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-768x487.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-180x114.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-537x340.png 537w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption-297x188.png 297w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/29-figure-22-AES-decryption.png 1446w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 29</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The AES decryption routines</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>This encryption of the underlying malware is typically employed to bypass detection by endpoint security controls such as antivirus programs. Many ‘crypter’ tools, as they are known, are available for purchase or trade on various hacking forums.</p>



<p>Of particular note, this decrypting stub code retained its PDB (short for Program Database) information. PDB file references are common in .Net applications when compiled in ‘debug’ mode, and they frequently reveal the original file path of the application source code on the developer’s computer.</p>



<p>This executable revealed the following PDB file path:</p>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">c:usersmr.tekidedocumentsvisual studio 2013projectspaccryptnano core dehgani -vdswindowsapplication2objdebuglaunch manager.pdb</p>



<p>This PDB string indicates that ‘mr.tekide’ was the username of the developer who compiled this particular stub, and further that it was compiled as part of a Visual Studio project named ‘paccryptnano core dehgani -vds’. In addition, a single subroutine found inside the decrypting stub was named ‘tekide’. The relevance of this PDB string was discussed above in <strong>Part 5: Attribution</strong>.</p>



<p>In order to obtain the intended malware payload from this decrypting stub executable, we created a small .Net application to mimic the decryption steps and output the file to disk. Once complete, we obtained a malicious executable with hashes:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>MD5</th><th>SHA256</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>dd5bedd915967c5efe00733cf7478cb4</td><td>a9db5a548ea17d6606bfbdb20306a3a08b38dbfe720f9f709f4d3369288be104</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 5</strong></figcaption></figure>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Original NanoCore binary</h4>



<p>Now that we have arrived at the original NanoCore binary, we can examine the configuration as specified by the operator. In order to extract the configuration settings from this copy of NanoCore, we used Kevin Breen’s <a href="https://github.com/kevthehermit/RATDecoders">RATDecoders</a>.</p>



<p>Using Breen’s tool we arrived at the following configuration:</p>

class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 30</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The NanoCore configuration, using Kevin Breen’s RATDecoders</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Notably, 88.198.222[.]163 port 8081 is the command and control channel for this malware. As noted in Part 1, the same IP was also present in the seeding e-mail header.</p>



<p><a name="appendix-a-dropper-2"></a></p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Dropper Doc 2</h3>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">assadcrimes1.ppsx<br>MD5:F1F84EA3229DCA0CCACB7381A2F49F99</p>



<p>This PowerPoint document leverages CVE-2014-4114, a vulnerability in the OLE packager component of the Windows operating system. As described in <a href="https://citizenlab.ca/2015/06/targeted-attacks-against-tibetan-and-hong-kong-groups-exploiting-cve-2014-4114/">previous reporting</a>, this vulnerability causes a file embedded within the PowerPoint document to be copied to disk and executed silently on vulnerable systems.</p>



<p>The document under examination drops a file named <em>dvm.gif</em> to disk, renames it to <em>dvm.exe</em> and then executes it. This dvm.exe is the same packed and crypted copy of NanoCore as retrieved and executed by the .Net downloader described in the previous section.</p>



<p><a name="appendix-a-dropper-3"></a></p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Dropper Doc 3</h3>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">assadcrimes.info.ppsx<br>MD5: 30BB678DB3AD0140FC33ACD9803385C3</p>



<p>This malicious PowerPoint document uses the same weaponization method described above with respect to Dropper Doc 1. The executable is embedded as an OLE package object, and subsequently executed using animation actions within the PowerPoint slideshow.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE.png"><img decoding="async" width="700" height="506" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAArwAAAH6AQMAAADfnQ3sAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAEJJREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14ND2AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAcCWv6gABezx5YQAAAABJRU5ErkJggg==" alt="Figure 24: The malicious OLE Package, visible when editing the PPSX" class="wp-image-27815 lazyload" title="Figure 31"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE.png 700w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-470x340.png 470w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-275x199.png 275w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="700" data-eio-rheight="506" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="700" height="506" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE.png" alt="Figure 24: The malicious OLE Package, visible when editing the PPSX" class="wp-image-27815" title="Figure 31" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE.png 700w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-300x217.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-180x130.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-470x340.png 470w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/31-figure-24-OLE-275x199.png 275w" sizes="(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 31</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The malicious OLE Package, visible when editing the PPSX</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>As with Dropper Doc 1, activation of the OLE Package object saves the embedded executable to disk as %TEMP%putty.exe, then executes it. This file is a .Net application employing the same layers and methods of packing and crypting as seen in the payloads delivered by Dropper Docs 1 and 2. However, the ultimate malware payload in this case is njRat, another well-known RAT tool.</p>



<p>After unpacking the OLE embedded executable putty.exe, we again arrive at a decrypting stub file which will AES decrypt a base64 string variable and run it from memory. The hashes of this file are:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>MD5</th><th>SHA256</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>6161083021b695814434450c1882f9f3</td><td>d72676bbf8de82486c3cebfdad2961cc68a6b564a43f9f987c95320fcd6a330a</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 6</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>Similar to the case of Dropper Doc 1 above, we find a PDB entry present in the decrypting stub executable:</p>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">C:Usersmr.tekideDocumentsVisual Studio 2013Projectspaccrypt11njratmaliipaccryptalipnahzadeobjDebugLManager.pdb</p>



<p>Again we can observe the same username of ‘<em>mr.tekide</em>’ in the project source code path within the PDB string. Further, we note the development path components <em>paccrypt11njratmalii</em> and <em>paccryptalipnahzade</em>.</p>



<p>To obtain the malicious njRat executable from this decrypting stub we used the same .Net program we built for use in the Dropper Doc 1 example above. The resulting njRat binary had the following hashes:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>MD5</th><th>SHA256</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>b4121c3a1892332402000ef0d587c0ee</td><td>1a287331e2bfb4df9cfe2dab1b77c9b5522e923e52998a2b1934ed8a8e52f3a8</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 7</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>Interestingly, the njRat executable appears to have been compiled from source by the same user who compiled the crypter described above. Note the PDB strings found inside the njRat executable:</p>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">C:Usersmr.tekideDocumentsVisual Studio 2013Projectsnjrat7stubsoures &#8211; Copynjrat7stubsouresobjDebugdvvm.pdb</p>



<p>A quick look at the configuration data embedded within this njRat binary reveals the command and control IP address and port:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/32-figure-25-njrat-config.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="882" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAABAAAAANyAQMAAADGniuEAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAIRJREFUeNrtwQEBAAAAgiD/r25IQAEAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAvBu8gQABc/yd/wAAAABJRU5ErkJggg==" alt="Figure 25: The njRAT configuration, showing the C2 and port." class="wp-image-27818 lazyload" title="Figure 32"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/32-figure-25-njrat-config-1024x882.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/32-figure-25-njrat-config-1024x882.png <h3 class="wp-block-heading">Decoy Dropper 4</h3>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">alshohadaa alatfal.exe<br>MD5: 2FC276E1C06C3C78C6D7B66A141213BE</p>



<p>This file is a .Net application designed to act as a decoy by displaying a window depicting images of dead children (see Figure: 5). While displaying these images, the decoy application also silently extracts an executable file from the .Net assembly’s resource section, copies it to <em>%TEMP%dvm.exe</em>, and then launches a new process from this newly created file. See Figure 26 below:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/33-figure-26-malware-dropping.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="460" class="wp-element-caption">Malware dropping code inside the Decoy application</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>The dropper also includes a PDB reference:</p>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">C:usersenterokdesktopslideshowslideshowobjx86debugalshohadaa alatfal.pdb</p>



<p>The dvm.exe file is itself a .Net executable which is packed using the same .Net packer used above in the cases of Dropper Docs 1 &#8211; 3. Once unpacked, the resulting file is the same crypted .Net application analysed above from Dropper Doc 3, having MD5 hash 6161083021b695814434450c1882f9f3, and containing the njRat payload.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Malware Infrastructure</h3>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Command and Control Server</h4>



<p>Each of the three distinct RAT tools used by Group5 (njRAT, NanoCore RAT, and DroidJack) were configured to communicate with a single command and control server operating on IP address 88.198.222[.]163.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>IP</th><th>Reverse DNS PTR</th><th>Assignee</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>88.198.222[.]163</td><td>static.88-198-222-163.clients.your-server.de</td><td>HETZNER-RZ-NBG-BLK4<br>Hetzner Online GmbH</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 8</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>This server was the sole point of data exfiltration for each of the malware components. As detailed above for njRAT and NanoCore, and below in <a href="#appendix-b">Appendix B</a> for DroidJack, the TCP ports used for command and control for each of the RAT tools were as follows:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports.png"><img decoding="async" width="546" height="291" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAiIAAAEjAQMAAAA1zvhRAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAClJREFUGBntwQEBAAAAgqD+r3ZIwAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAADgUk+SAAEBpZs9AAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="Figure 27: RAT ports on the C2 server" class="wp-image-27820 lazyload" title="Figure 34"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports.png 546w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-300x160.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-180x96.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-297x158.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="546" data-eio-rheight="291" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="546" height="291" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports.png" alt="Figure 27: RAT ports on the C2 server" class="wp-image-27820" title="Figure 34" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports.png 546w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-300x160.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-180x96.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/34-figure-27-RAT-ports-297x158.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 546px) 100vw, 546px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 34</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">RAT ports on the C2 server</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Additionally, we believe a controller for yet another remote access trojan, XpertRAT, was also hosted on this IP in November 2015; however, we did not uncover any samples designed to communicate with this XpertRAT controller.</p>



<p>As noted in the above table, the IP address 88.198.222[.]163 is assigned to Hetzner Online, a Germany based web hosting provider. Hetzner offers web hosting services as well as virtual and dedicated server rentals. Contact was made with Hetzner technical personnel subsequent to the discovery of the malicious activity outlined in this report. A synopsis of this contact is provided in <a href="#appendix-f">Appendix F: Notification</a>.</p>



<p>Current data available for this IP address suggests that it was likely reprovisioned to a different Hetzner customer in early February 2016 at the latest, and then possibly again in May. A series of domain names associated with online multi-player games were directed to this Hetzner IP, one of which was apparently hosting <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/en/file/dd55d13621a905d0f4c4dfe84c4d2b68769debe7a79cf1ce514940306da47c25/analysis/">a malicious HTML document</a>.</p>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Assadcrimes Web Hosting</h4>



<p>The assadcrimes[.]info domain name was registered in June 2015, but it remained parked until early October, at which time it was migrated to an Iran-based shared web hosting provider named Hostnegar. This action coincided with the delivery of the initial e-mails outlined in <strong>Part 1</strong>.</p>



<p>The assadcrimes[.]info website was hosted on a shared hosting platform, and as such the IP address associated was also shared by a significant number of other, unrelated, websites.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>IP</th><th>Reverse DNS PTR</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>212.7.195[.]171</td><td>server22.rayanegarco[.]com</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 9</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>Headers from the initial e-mail are shown below in Figure 28. These headers indicate that the initial e-mail was most likely sent using the Horde webmail application running on the web hosting server. Furthermore, the headers indicate that the sender was accessing the webmail application from the IP address of the command and control server discussed above.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/35-figure-28-headers.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="241" https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/35-figure-28-headers-768x181.png 768w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/35-figure-28-headers-180x42.png 180w, <p>Finally, available domain name service data indicates that the assadcrimes[.]info domain name was moved back to its original parked location on May 4, 2016.<br><a name="appendix-b"></a></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Appendix B: Android Malware Analysis</h2>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">The Malicious APK &#8211; Overall Description</h3>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAdIAAAMtAQMAAAAR9WKQAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAEZJREFUeNrtwTEBAAAAwqD1T+1vBqAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAHgDvowAAfPcnKwAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="" title="Figure 36 A" data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/36a-figure-29-malicious-app.png" class="lazyload" data-eio-rwidth="466" data-eio-rheight="813" /><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/36a-figure-29-malicious-app.png" alt="" title="Figure 36 A" data-eio="l" /></noscript><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 36 A</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The malicious application is installed, and appears in the Apps tab</figcaption></div></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAY8AAAK8AQMAAAAtUqE6AAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAADlJREFUeNrtwTEBAAAAwqD1T20KP6AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAA4GuLdAABbXpgzgAAAABJRU5ErkJggg==" alt="" title="Figure 36 B" data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/36a-figure-29-hidden.png" class="lazyload" data-eio-rwidth="399" data-eio-rheight="700" /><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/36a-figure-29-hidden.png" alt="" title="Figure 36 B" data-eio="l" /></noscript><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 36 B</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The malicious application is hidden from the Apps list from the Drawer</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Upon execution, the malware is installed and then hidden from the list of installed applications in order to remain covert.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/37-figure-30-malicious-app-error.png"><img decoding="async" width="550" height="973" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAiYAAAPNAQMAAACH9DPwAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAFhJREFUeNrtwQEBAAAAgiD/r25IQAEAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAN8GCh0AAZ3CJcwAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="Figure 30 : The malicious app gives an error ‘App isn’t installed’ when the user tries to open the malware before it disappears from the list. <p>After the installation, the Application icon will be removed from the installed applications list, yet it will still be running in the background.</p>



<p>The APK package in question had the following characteristics:</p>



<p class="has-white-background-color has-background has-size-14-font-size">Adobe_Flash_Player.apk<br>MD5: 8EBEB3F91CDA8E985A9C61BEB8CDDE9D</p>



<p>This APK is an instance of DroidJack. <a href="http://www.symantec.com/connect/blogs/droidjack-rat-tale-how-budding-entrepreneurism-can-turn-cybercrime">According to Symantec</a>, this application evolved from an older codebase known as SandroRAT.</p>



<p>The discovered APK sample also contains references to both names, as shown in Figure 38 below:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAKEAAACDAQMAAADf++nIAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAABlJREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14Gf2AAAMAhC0IAAUXnn7gAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="" title="Figure 38" data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/38a-figure-31-references.png" class="lazyload" data-eio-rwidth="161" data-eio-rheight="131" /><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/38a-figure-31-references.png" alt="" title="Figure 38" data-eio="l" /></noscript><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 38</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">References to both DroidJack and SandroRat as seen in the source code</figcaption></div></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAUMAAACNAQMAAAAXaIo0AAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAABtJREFUGBntwQEBAAAAgqD+r3ZIwAAAAAAA4FIXIgAB2wbYPQAAAABJRU5ErkJggg==" alt="" data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/38b-figure-31-references.png" class="lazyload" data-eio-rwidth="323" data-eio-rheight="141" /><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/38b-figure-31-references.png" alt="" data-eio="l" /></noscript></figure>



<p>The APK Manifest file reveals important information about the sample’s capabilities and the intentions of its operator. The Android operating system requires information from the Manifest file before the application can execute. This application will request the following permissions and use the following features from the device:</p>

<p>In the Android system, Activities are components typically used to let the user of the device perform an action. The Main Activity is also defined in the Manifest, pictured in Figure 33.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity.png"><img decoding="async" width="745" height="96" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAukAAABgAQMAAACaKn5xAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAB9JREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14KP2AAAAAAAAAAAMBNI6AAAYaklNQAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="Figure 33: Main activity defined in the manifest" class="wp-image-27830 lazyload" title="Figure 40"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity.png 745w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-300x39.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-180x23.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-605x78.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-297x38.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="745" data-eio-rheight="96" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="745" height="96" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity.png" alt="Figure 33: Main activity defined in the manifest" class="wp-image-27830" title="Figure 40" https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-180x23.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-605x78.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/40-figure-33-main-activity-297x38.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 745px) 100vw, 745px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 40</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Main activity defined in the manifest</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>In this case, the Main Activity is designed to start the Controller as a Service and finish. The controller will be discussed in more detail in the next section.</p>



<p>Android applications can also have Services and Receivers defined. Services are used for background operations while Receivers define the types of broadcast messages the application can receive from other applications as well as the device. These messages are known as Intents.</p>



<p>This APK sample enables several services including “Controller,” “GPSLocation” and “Toaster” (See Figure 34).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity.png"><img decoding="async" width="658" height="49" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAApIAAAAxAQMAAABTUdtMAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAABpJREFUWMPtwQEBAAAAgiD/r25IQAEAAACPBhAUAAG8YRXBAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="Figure 34: Services enabled by the APK" class="wp-image-27831 lazyload" title="Figure 41"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity.png 658w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-300x22.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-180x13.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-605x45.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-297x22.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="658" data-eio-rheight="49" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="658" height="49" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity.png" alt="Figure 34: Services enabled by the APK" class="wp-image-27831" title="Figure 41" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity.png 658w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-300x22.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-180x13.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-605x45.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/41-figure-34-main-activity-297x22.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 658px) 100vw, 658px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 41</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Services enabled by the APK</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>The Controller class, referred to by the Main Activity and started as a service on the device, handles the malware operator’s interaction with the application while the GPSLocation class is responsible for obtaining the GPS position from the device’s LocationManager. The Toaster class is not implemented in this APK; however, it is implemented in older SandroRAT samples.</p>



<p>The APK file has several Receiver classes defined to handle specific messages from the device (See Figure 42).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver.png"><img decoding="async" width="568" height="178" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAjgAAACyAQMAAAB1DyciAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAACNJREFUaN7twQENAAAAwqD3T20ON6AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAB4MjIQAAE6FKqiAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="Figure 35: Defined receiver classes" class="wp-image-27832 lazyload" title="Figure 42"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver.png 568w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-300x94.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-180x56.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-297x93.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="568" data-eio-rheight="178" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="568" height="178" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver.png" alt="Figure 35: Defined receiver classes" class="wp-image-27832" title="Figure 42" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver.png 568w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-300x94.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-180x56.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/42-figure-35-defined-receiver-297x93.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 568px) 100vw, 568px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 42</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Defined receiver classes</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>Receiver Intent</th><th>Usage</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Connectivity Change</td><td>Allows the application to monitor any connectivity changes, including moving between mobile data and Wi-Fi. The constant value is set every time a change occurs.</td></tr><tr><td>Boot Completed</td><td>Allows the application to re-connect when the device restarts. The constant value is broadcast when the device finishes booting.</td></tr><tr><td>Phone State</td><td>Allows the application to monitor incoming calls. The constant value is set when the call state is changed.</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 10</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>The Connector Receiver simply starts the Controller Service when the phone boots allowing the malware to run in the background upon start up.</p>



<p>The CallListener Receiver allows the operator to log when the target makes calls, and record calls (if the operator has enabled it) as an .amr file that can then be sent to the command and control server.</p>



<p>Lastly, in the Manifest file, the Application enables two additional Activities, “CAMSNAP” and “VIDEOCAP,” as shown in Figure 36.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video.png"><img decoding="async" width="580" height="260" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAkQAAAEEAQMAAADznrnoAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAClJREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14ND2AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAABwKEsoAAHArKAuAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="Figure 36: Activities enabling camera and video capture" class="wp-image-27833 lazyload" title="Figure 43"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video.png 580w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-300x134.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-180x81.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-297x133.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="580" data-eio-rheight="260" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="580" height="260" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video.png" alt="Figure 36: Activities enabling camera and video capture" class="wp-image-27833" title="Figure 43" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video.png 580w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-300x134.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-180x81.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/43-figure-36-camera-video-297x133.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 580px) 100vw, 580px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 43</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Activities enabling camera and video capture</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>These allow the operator to use the infected device’s camera to take pictures and record video. This activity is hidden from the victim using a translucent theme.</p>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">The Malicious APK &#8211; The Controller</h4>



<p>As previously mentioned, the Controller class is ultimately responsible for the rest of the functionality. The instance we analyzed was configured to use the same host as the Windows malware for command and control communication: 88.198.222[.]163.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack.png"><img decoding="async" width="337" height="120" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAVEAAAB4AQMAAABxbFNLAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAABxJREFUWMPtwTEBAAAAwqD1T20MH6AAAAAAAD4GFKAAAfHhVf8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt="Figure 37: DroidJack configuration showing that it shares a host with the other Group5 malware" class="wp-image-27834 lazyload" title="Figure 44"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack.png 337w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-300x107.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-180x64.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-297x106.png 297w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="337" data-eio-rheight="120" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="337" height="120" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack.png" alt="Figure 37: DroidJack configuration showing that it shares a host with the other Group5 malware" class="wp-image-27834" title="Figure 44" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack.png 337w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-300x107.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-180x64.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/44-figure-37-droidjack-297x106.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 337px) 100vw, 337px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 44</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">DroidJack configuration showing that it shares a host with the other Group5 malware</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>We were able to install a test instance to learn how the malware’s operator could surveil victims. It is clear that the operator would have nearly full access to the victim’s information.</p>



<p>Features offered include:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>File browsing</li>



<li>SMS and call logging</li>



<li>Contacts</li>



<li>Browser history</li>



<li>Application Manager</li>



<li>Location history</li>



<li>WhatsApp Reader (only works on rooted devices)</li>



<li>Remote camera and microphone</li>
</ul>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignnone"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/45-figure-38-droidjack-whatsapp.png"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="631" class="wp-element-caption">DroidJack browsing WhatsApp logs for an infected device.</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Some features will only work on rooted devices. For example, the ability to read WhatsApp messages requires the victim’s device to be rooted. Android apps are unable to access the data from other applications unless they are signed with the same certificate or if the app has been given permission to execute commands as root. If DroidJack is able to acquire root access it can then upload the database on the device where WhatsApp stores its message history.<br><a name="appendix-c"></a></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Appendix C: Mr. Tekide</h2>



<p>This appendix provides more context on Mr. Tekide, first delving into how we have identified his crypter (PAC Crypt) in strings in the binaries, and second highlighting the results of open source searching for his aliases and related strings.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Sample Correlation With PDB Strings</h3>



<p>In the Group5 malware samples, we have several PDB file references that suggest that the crypter used with the two distinct RAT tools (njRat and NanoCore) was Mr. Tekide’s ‘PAC Crypt’. For the njRat sample from Dropper Doc 3, we can see the malware stub was compiled by ‘mr.tekide’ as well.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>Reference: Doc Dropper 1 Crypter</th><th>MD5: a4f1f4921bb11ff9d22fad89b19b155d</th><th>Compile Time: 9/30/2015 00:02:51</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">c:usersmr.tekidedocumentsvisual studio 2013projectspaccryptnano core dehgani -vdswindowsapplication2objdebuglaunch manager.pdb</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 11</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>Reference: Doc Dropper 3 Crypter</th><th>MD5:6161083021b695814434450c1882f9f3</th><th>Compile Time: 10/6/2015 02:13:45</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">C:Usersmr.tekideDocumentsVisual Studio 2013Projectspaccrypt11njratmaliipaccryptalipnahzadeobjDebugLManager.pdb</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 12</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><thead><tr><th>Reference: Doc Dropper 3 njRat Payload</th><th>MD5:b4121c3a1892332402000ef0d587c0ee</th><th>Compile Time: 10/6/2015 01:23:31</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">C:Usersmr.tekideDocumentsVisual Studio 2013Projectsnjrat7stubsoures &#8211; Copynjrat7stubsouresobjDebugdvvm.pdb</td></tr></tbody></table><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><strong>Table 13</strong></figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>The Visual Studio project folders listed above suggest the particular version of PAC Crypt compiled by Mr. Tekide was being prepared in one case for an njRat payload, and another for a NanoCore payload. The strings ‘dehgani –vds’, ‘malii’ and ‘alipnahzade’ may have additional significance or relevance.</p>



<p>We conducted searches across online malware repositories and analysis services (such as VirusTotal, Malwr, and TotalHash) in an effort to acquire additional data relating to the use of PAC Crypt. These searches revealed very little in relation to PAC Crypt specifically, so we instead examined the data for instances of ‘tekide’ related strings found in PDB files.</p>



<p>It is our hope that the data or avenue of investigation presented below may be of value to other researchers.</p>



<p>The results we examined contained over 200 samples which we then clustered into sets based on compile time and PDB reference as shown in the table below:</p>

47" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/47a-compile-time-pdb-full.png 743w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/47a-compile-time-pdb-full-300x147.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/47a-compile-time-pdb-full-180x88.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/47a-compile-time-pdb-full-605x296.png 605w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/47a-compile-time-pdb-full-297x145.png 297w" sizes="(max-width: 743px) 100vw, 743px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 47</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Compile time / PDB references were also observed in singular instances</figcaption></div></figure>



<p>Keeping in mind the limitations of reliance on compile times, we nevertheless were able to compare the noted compile times against the first time samples appeared in common malware repositories such as VirusTotal, Malwr, and TotalHash. In most instances, samples began to appear in malware repositories within hours of the files being compiled. Dynamic analysis of the samples in these sets revealed multiple different payloads and C2 configurations. For example, analysis of the samples in Set A yielded the following payloads and configurations:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignleft"><a href="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples.png"><img decoding="async" width="754" height="523" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAvIAAAILAQMAAACwyOreAAAAA1BMVEVHcEyC+tLSAAAAAXRSTlMAQObYZgAAAEdJREFUGBntwTEBAAAAwiD7p14ND2AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAABwLMQgAAGZjAeKAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt="analysis of the samples in Set A yielded the following payloads and configurations" class="wp-image-27840 lazyload" title="Figure 48"   data-src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples.png" data-srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples.png 754w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-300x208.png 300w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-180x125.png 180w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-490x340.png 490w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-287x199.png 287w" data-sizes="auto" data-eio-rwidth="754" data-eio-rheight="523" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="754" height="523" src="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples.png" alt="analysis of the samples in Set A yielded the following payloads and configurations" class="wp-image-27840" title="Figure 48" srcset="https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples.png 754w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-490x340.png 490w, https://citizenlab.ca/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/48-analysis-samples-287x199.png 287w" sizes="(max-width: 754px) 100vw, 754px" data-eio="l" /></noscript></a><div class="wp-block-image__meta"><div class="wp-block-image__title">Figure 48</div><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Analysis of the samples in Set A yielded the following payloads and configurations</figcaption></div></figure>



<p></p>



<p>Finally, analysis of the compile times observed across the acquired samples suggest a period of activity falling in the latter half of 2014. There are many possible explanations as to why so few samples were observed with compile times beyond 2014: conscious removal of PDB information, a change in personal circumstances, or possibly even a shift to less public malware development activities.</p>



<h4 class="wp-block-heading">Mr. Tekide on the Internet</h4>



<p>Mr. Tekide maintains a visible profile across various malware related web forums, as well as on social media. Searches conducted for this alias provided numerous results which reveal a consistent use of the Mr. Tekide name and avatar, as shown in the images below.</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>A link found on the ‘Contact’ page of the crypter[.]ir website led to a <a href="https://www.facebook.com/tekide">Facebook profile</a> in the name of ‘Pezhman Blackhat.’ In addition to this Facebook profile, we also identified a <a href="https://ir.linkedin.com/in/pezhman-tekide-a87054bb">LinkedIn profile</a> in which he refers to himself as a &#8216;crypter,&#8217; and states that he works for the ashiyane digital security team. He also maintains an <a href="https://www.instagram.com/tekide/">Instagram profile</a>.</p>

<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><a name="appendix-f"></a>Appendix F: Notification</h2>



<p>On April 12, 2016 we contacted Hetzner via e-mail as well as their abuse form, and informed them that the server was being used to host malware. We also provided network logs as well as a malware sample. We subsequently followed up with two phone calls. On a telephone call, a Hetzner representative refused to investigate, stating that they would take no investigative action before sharing the content of our complaint with the customer, who would then have 24 hours to take action. When we suggested that this might result in the deletion of evidence, and highlighted the special nature of the case, the representative refused any further action.<br>Footnotes<a name="1"></a><sup>1</sup> Noura has given her permission for us to disclose her role in this case, and use her photograph.<br><a name="2"></a><sup>2</sup> Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 6.3; rv:39.0) Gecko/20100101 Firefox/39.0<br><a name="3"></a><sup>3</sup> http://ashiyane[.]org/forums<br><a name="4"></a><sup>4</sup> This technique has been <a href="http://phishme.com/powerpoint-and-custom-actions/">documented previously</a>.</p>
		</div>

							<a href="https://citizenlab.ca/research/from-protest-to-peril-cellebrite-used-against-jordanian-civil-society/">
					<h2 class="content-card__title">From Protest to Peril</h2>
					<h3 class="content-card__subtitle">Cellebrite Used Against Jordanian Civil Society</h3>
				</a>
					</header>
		<div class="content-card__excerpt">
			<p>Through a multi-year investigation, we find that the Jordanian security apparatus has deployed forensic extraction products manufactured by Cellebrite against civil society devices. We release these findings alongside reporting from the Organized Crime and Corruption Reporting Project (OCCRP) which includes interviews with a few of the victims.</p>
		</div>

		<div class="content-card__date-focus-areas-wrapper">
			<div class="content-card__date">
				January 22, 2026			</div>

						<a class="single__focus-area" href="https://citizenlab.ca/focus-area/targeted-surveillance/">
							Targeted Surveillance						</a>
									</div>
					</div>
	</div>
